                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20battery">Na-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Polymer+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_polymer%20anode">polymer anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Moderate+Pyrolysis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_moderate%20pyrolysis">moderate pyrolysis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electronic+Conductivity&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electronic%20conductivity">electronic conductivity</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High-rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high-rate%20capability">high-rate capability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrastable+Cyclability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrastable%20cyclability">ultrastable cyclability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been the leading energy-storage devices for various application including portable electronic products, electric vehicles, and grid energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the very limited Li reserve (0.0017 wt % in Earth’s crust) and its uneven distribution causes serious concern for its sustainability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> Accordingly, alternative battery systems based on abundant recourse are being intensively pursued. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> In this regard, sodium-ion battery (NIB) was considered as the promising candidate due to not only the resource abundance and low cost of Na element but also the referential manufactured procedures from LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 8 to 13">(8−13)</a> To build high-performance NIBs, seeking anode materials with high capacity, superior rate capability, and extraordinary cycling stability is critical, especially if the expansion of NIBs applications to different areas like portable devices and electric vehicles is expected. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> However, the development of such reliable NIBs anodes remained a significant challenge at this stage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> The direct transferring of LIBs anode materials to NIBs anode materials, typically the commercial graphite, has failed so far. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Although other candidates like amorphous carbon, alloys, Na metal, and metal compounds have showed promising potential for Na storage, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> they were challenged by the unsatisfactory rate capability and inferior cyclability, which probably were induced by the repeated intercalation/deintercalation of the larger-size Na<sup>+</sup> (1.94 Å, in contrast to 1.36 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Owing to the abundant resource, environmental sustainability, and enormous room for molecular-level designability and functionalization, the organic anode materials are now drawing intense research interest for NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> However, the organic anodes like redox-active molecules are usually challenged by the solubility issues and the inferior electronic conductivity. The low electronic conductivity usually made large amounts of conductive additive (20–50 wt %) requisite for preparing the working electrodes, accordingly lowering the energy density of the electrode seriously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 and 25">(23,25)</a> Building redox-active molecules into a polymeric structure, like covalent organic polymers (COPs), covalent organic frameworks (COFs), and metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), have been identified as effective strategies for fighting against the solubility of the molecules electrode in organic electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> Unfortunately, the development of polymeric electrodes with both high redox activity and high electronic conductivity remains a formidable challenge. For example, Shi et al. identified highly stable nitrogen-rich COFs materials for Na storage, but 40 wt % Super P is needed as conductive additive to ensure the conductivity of the electrode film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> It is noted that the high fraction of conductive carbon addition would seriously harm the device’s energy density. Meanwhile, the use of large amounts of conductive carbon with high specific surface area usually induces high electrode porosity, which would lower the Coulombic efficiency and demand more electrolyte to ensure the ionic conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> It thus reminded us that a reasonable design on the polymer electrodes should take an overall consideration of both the electrochemical performances and their electronic conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Among various polymer anode candidates, the N-rich conjugated organic polymers, typically, polymeric carbon nitride (PCN), have emerged as the promising candidates for NIBs anode due to its structure flexibility, chemical stability, and potentially high redox activity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 31 to 33">(31−33)</a> The PCN materials feature layer architecture with two-dimensional π conjugated structure and abundant built-in redox-active group such as C═N and N═N, which are capable of storing Na<sup>+</sup> to get a high capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref27 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref27 ref32" aria-label="reference 22, 27 and 32">(22,27,32)</a> Meanwhile, the spread conjugated skeleton and the larger layer space were expected to facilitate the large-size Na<sup>+</sup> transfer and enable the reliable structural stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 31, 34 and 35">(31,34,35)</a> Unfortunately, similar to most organic electrodes, the PCN was challenged by the inherently low electronic conductivity, sluggish electrode kinetics, and limited cycling life, although combining the PCN with the conductive carbon to build the PCN/carbon composites has been demonstrated an effective route to improve the electronic conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 32, 33 and 36 to 38">(32,33,36−38)</a> However, this strategy is tedious in operation, low in yield, and still unable to avoid the request of a large fraction of carbon additives.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this contribution, we demonstrated that a moderate pyrolysis of a C<sub>2</sub>N-based PCN anode not only can enhance its electronic conductivity but also can tune its microstructures to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transfer/storage and sustain the repeated sodiation/desodiation. Such a moderate-pyrolysis process featured the controlling of heat treatment at a relatively low temperature (e.g., 350 °C). Specifically, we identified that the moderate pyrolysis of the PCN would reduce its bandgap to enhance the electronic conductivity. Equally importantly, such moderate pyrolysis would increase the structural disorder and expand the layer distance, which are beneficial for the fast transfer and efficient storage of large-size Na<sup>+</sup>. When used as NIBs anode material with 10 wt % conductive carbon added for preparing the electrode, the moderate-pyrolysis PCN can deliver an extraordinary electrochemical performance with a high reversible capacity (351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C), a superior rate capability (95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20C), and an ultrastable cyclability (&lt;0.0018% capacity decay per cycle over 6500 cycles at 2C). Moreover, a sodium-ion full cell constructed with this moderate-pyrolysis PCN anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> could also promise high reversible capacity and impressive cyclability. This contribution opens a facile and effective avenue in building robust redox-active polymer with impressive electronic conductivity and favored microstructure for high-performance Na<sup>+</sup> storage.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> Figure S1 are schemes of the synthesis and moderate-pyrolysis route for the preparation of C<sub>2</sub>N-based PCN anodes. The pristine PCN was first synthesized via a simple polycondensation of hexaketocyclohexane (HKH) octahydrate and hexaaminobenzene (HAB) trihydrochloride. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The collected pristine product (PCN-Pristine) was dried at 120 °C in vacuum for 24 h and then modified by a moderate-pyrolysis treatment at 350 °C under Ar atmosphere to get the targeted product (PCN-350). The product yield of the targeted PCN-350 is around 82 wt %. It is noted that this moderate-pyrolysis strategy is simple and highly scalable but was paid less attention to  the previous fabrication of polymer electrode. Our interest in such moderate-pyrolysis treatment was initially sparked by a moderate pyrolysis being expected to only modify the microstructure of the polymeric framework rather than majorly destroying the characters and structure of the polymer. Such a moderate-pyrolysis process is quite different from widely reported carbonization by high temperature (e.g., &gt;700 °C), which would usually induce serious deterioration of the polymers and transfer them into carbonaceous substances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Surprisingly, when used as NIBs anode with only 10 wt % Super P added for preparing the electrode film, the PCN-350 sample showed a superior Na-storage reversible capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C (1C = 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b), which is an over 15-fold increase compared to that of the PCN-pristine anode. This capacity (351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) is among one of the highest values when compared with the previously reported organic anodes in NIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). To learn more about the moderate-pyrolysis-enabled Na-storage activity, temperature-control experiments were performed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c illustrated their reversible specific capacity. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> showed the typical discharge/charge curves of the PCN samples heated by different temperatures (200, 350, 500, and 700 °C). With the increasing of the pyrolysis temperature, we did not see a monotonic change of the specific capacity while the PCN-350 sample showed the highest value. A low-temperature (e.g., 200 °C) treatment has almost no effect on enhancing the Na-storage capability. The resultant PCN-200 anode delivered almost the same low specific capacity (∼20 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) as the PCN-Pristine. Conversely, a higher-temperature treatment at 500 or 700 °C led to the decrease of Na-storage capability. The specific capacities for PCN-500 and PCN-700 are 263 and 172 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are much lower than the capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for PCN-350. It exemplified the key of identifying a suitable temperature for such a moderate-pyrolysis strategy. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) analyses were performed to evaluate the electrochemical activity of the temperature-controlled PCNs samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), which revealed the highest redox activity of the PCN-350 for Na storage. The initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of PCN-350, PCN-500, and PCN-700 are 61.5%, 60.3%, and 42.9%. The loss of irreversible discharged capacity in the initial cycle is probably related to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). We expected that the optimization of the electrolytes would effectively enhance the ICE of the anodes in our future studies.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of PCN-Pristine and PCN-350 via a moderate pyrolysis. (b) Typical discharge/charge profiles of PCN-Pristine and PCN-350 in half-cells. (c) Specific capacity for the PCN anodes with temperature control including PCN-Pristine, PCN-200, PCN-350, PCN-500, and PCN-700.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Intrigued by the significant improvement of Na-storage activity for such moderate-pyrolysis-modified PCN anode, we performed various characterizations to probe its structure variation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a showed the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of the PCN-350 which featured a micro-/nanosheet morphology. To have a close look at the morphologies of the PCN materials before and after pyrolysis, the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) characterization was carried out. Panels b–d of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> are the HRTEM images for PCN-Pristine, PCN-350, and PCN-700, respectively, which revealed similar amorphous states without obvious lattice stripes for these three samples. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e recorded the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of these three samples. The sharp peaks centered around 27.2° for PCN-Pristine agree well with the typical (002) crystal planes of the C<sub>2</sub>N framework. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> After the moderate pyrolysis at 350 °C, the peaks tune weaker with a broader half-peak width and are centered around 26.3°, suggesting the increase in both disorder degree and the structure defect for PCN-350. The calculated D<sub>002</sub> interlayer distance for PCN-350 is 3.35 Å, which is larger than that of 3.28 Å for PCN-Pristine. The enhanced disorder degree and larger distance are desirable structural characters for facilitating the transfer and storage of metal ion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> For the PCN-700 sample, a visible new peak around 42.5° emerged, which could be ascribed to the (100) peak of carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> suggesting the high-temperature carbonization would tune the PCN into carbonaceous materials.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural characterizations of PCN-Pristine, PCN-350, and PCN-700. (a) SEM images of PCN-350, which showed a micro-/nanosheet morphology. (b–d) HRTEM images. (e) XRD patterns. (f) Raman spectra. (g–j) Elemental mapping of a randomly selected region of PCN-350.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectroscopy and Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectroscopy were also used to probe structure variation of the PCN samples. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref43" aria-label="reference 27 and 43">(27,43)</a> The signals at 1511, 1375, 663, and 460 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the Raman spectrogram for the PCN-Pristine sample are the typical characteristics for the C<sub>2</sub>N-based framework (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f), which is consistent with previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> These characteristic signals in the Raman spectrogram show negligible change in the PCN-350 sample, suggesting the moderate-pyrolysis treatment at 350 °C would not seriously destroy the structure and characters of the PCN framework. On the contrary, for the PCN-700 sample, the signals at 663 and 460 cm<sup>–1</sup> vanished and the peak at 1511 cm<sup>–1</sup> shifted to 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which is fit with the G band of graphited carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 and 44">(42,44)</a> It suggested that the high-temperature pyrolysis would destroy the PCN framework and transferred it into amorphous carbon materials, which is well in accord with the XRD analysis results. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> recorded the FTIR spectra for the temperature-dependent samples, showing the key functional groups for C<sub>2</sub>N framework including -C═C- (1634 cm<sup>–1</sup>), -C–N- (1522 cm<sup>–1</sup>), and -C═N- (1399 cm<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> All these functional groups signals remained for PCN-350, although they turned weaker compared with that of the pristine sample, suggesting that the main structure and functional group for C<sub>2</sub>N framework was retained but some changes regarding the chemical cross-link inside the PCN occurred. The sum of the Raman spectrum, XRD, and FTIR analysis results highlighting a proper temperature (e.g., 350 °C) is critical for the moderate-pyrolysis treatment to tune the microstructures of the PCN with its polymeric framework being maintained. In contrast, a higher-temperature treatment would totally destroy the structure to carbon-based materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">To seek a better understanding of the change of structural details for these PCNs materials after moderate pyrolysis, a large variety of characterizations including elemental mapping, elemental analysis, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and thermogravimetry–mass spectrometry (TG-MS) were further carried out. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> and panels g–j of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> recorded the elemental mapping of PCN-Pristine and PCN-350, respectively. Both samples were composed of C, N, and O elements with a uniform distribution. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> illustrated the element content obtained by the elemental analysis, which showed that all PCN materials consist of C, H, N, and O. The O and H elements may originate from the edge groups of ketone and amine (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Interestingly, with the pyrolysis temperature increasing, both the O and H contents decreased monotonically, but the N contents rose first and then decreased, suggesting that the pyrolysis treatment would simultaneously induce further condensation of the polymer and a carbonization process if the temperature is too high (e.g., 700 °C). As a result, the C contents would increase monotonically with the increase of heated temperature. The XPS spectrum of the PCN material also comfirmed the existence of C, N, and O elements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>). The high-resolution N 1s region fitted onto two peaks located at 399 and 401 eV (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>), which are ascribed to C═N and C–N, repectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref45" aria-label="reference 39 and 45">(39,45)</a> The C–N peak for PCN-350 decreased, indicating further condensation of PCN with the conversion of diamine to pyrazine. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> To take a close look at the thermal degradation process of PCN, TG-MS was conducted to monitor the pyrolysis process and the released species. As illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the TG curve recorded the weight loss and the mass spectrometry showed the obvious production of H<sub>2</sub>O (<i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 18) and NH<sub>3</sub> (<i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 17) during the thermal treatment. H<sub>2</sub>O was probably produced from the condensation reaction between the terminal diketone and diamine groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The production of NH<sub>3</sub> might result from the condensation of two or three amine groups to form diphenylamine and triphenylamine linker, and the thermal decomposition of C<sub>2</sub>N. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Electronic conductivity is one of the most critical parameters for electrode materials when considering their electrochemical applications, particularly for organic electrodes whose electronic conductivity are inherently inferior. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> Interestingly, we notice that such a simple moderate-pyrolysis treatment is highly effective to enhance the electron conductivity of PCN anodes. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the electronic conductivity of PCN-pristine anode is low to 3.17 × 10<sup>–7</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>. Surprisingly, the PCN-350 electrode was able to deliver a value of 2.82 × 10<sup>–5</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>, which is a significant enhancement by 2 orders of magnitude compared to the pristine PCN materials. To have a better understanding of the reason for the conductivity enhancement, UV–visible spectrum analyses were carried out to track the optical bandgap, which is a well-known key parameter highly related to the electron transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b,c, the optical bandgap decreased from 1.33 eV for PCN-Pristine to 1.15 eV for PCN-350. The narrowing bandgap suggested the formation of a bigger conjugated structure during the moderated pyrolysis process, which enables fast electron transfer due to the much lower energy barrier, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> thus resulting in the much enhancement of the electronic conductivity for PCN-350.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Electrical conductivity of PCN-Pristine and PCN-350. (b–c) UV–vis spectrum and the Tauc plots of (<i>h</i>να) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> versus photon energy (<i>h</i>ν) for PCN-Pristine (b) and PCN-350 (c).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We demonstrated that the enhanced electronic conductivity and favorable microstructure characters (such as the increase of disorder degree, structure defect, and layer distance) of PCN-350 anode not only enable a high electrochemical activity for high capacity of Na<sup>+</sup> storage but also facilitate fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer for superior rate capability and ensure a reliable sodiation/dissodiation for ultrastable cyclability. It is noted that our test was based on an electrode with only 10 wt % super P adding as conductive addictive, rather than using massive conductive addictive (20–50 wt %; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a illustrated the C-rate performance tested at different charge–discharge current densities. PCN-350 can deliver high reversible capacities of 351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 296 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and 241 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C, 0.5C, and 2C, respectively. Even at the large current densities of 10C and 20C, PCN-350 still retains capacities of 151 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which showed a much better rate capability compared to control samples of PCN-Pristine and PCN-700. Such extraordinary high-rate performance is much better than that of the previously reported organic counterparts and carbon anodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) was used as a diagnostic tool to probe the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer in the surface of the anodes. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> is the Nyquist plots of PCN-Pristine, PCN-350, and PCN-700 with an equivalent circuit (inset of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). The charge-transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) for PCN-350 is 2688 Ω, which is lower than the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values for PCN-pristine (3293 Ω) and PCN-700 (3121 Ω), suggesting the faster charge transfer for PCN-350. Besides the superior high-rate capability, the prepared PCN-350 electrode also promises extraordinary cyclability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c illustrated the cycling performance of the PCN-350 anode tested at 2C after three cycles at 0.2C. PCN-350 was able to get a high-capacity retention of 88.5% after 6500 cycles (&lt;0.0018% capacity decay per cycle) with a high Coulombic efficiency &gt; 99.5%. This cyclability is one of the best performances as reported for the organic anodes so far from both reversible specific capacity and capacity retention points of view (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d,e).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical characterization of the PCN products in half-cell NIBs. (a) Rate capability test for PCN-pristine, PCN-350, and PCN-700. In this contribution, 1C = 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Comparison of the rate capability of PCN-350 samples and the reported organic counterparts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 32 and 49 to 58">(32,49−58)</a> (c) Cycling performance of the PCN-350 anode tested at 2C after three cycles at 0.2C. In the 2425th cycle, the Na metal as counter electrode was replaced by a fresh one. (d, e) Comparison of the reversible specific capacity (d) and capacity retention (e) of PCN-350 samples and the reported organic counterparts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 32 and 49 to 58">(32,49−58)</a> It is worth noting that the adding conductivity for the reported organic counterparts in (b) and (d, e) ranged from 20 to 50 wt %, but for PCNs was 10 wt %.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To have a better understanding of the fast and stable sodiation/desodiation process for this PCN-350 anode, the Na-storage behavior and the electrode structure evolution were investigated during the cycling. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests at scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were first carried out to probe the kinetics of the electrodes. The broad anodic and cathodic peaks feature pseudocapacitive behavior for Na<sup>+</sup> storage in PCN-350 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), which is in good accordance with the slope-dominated behavior for the charge/discharge curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). A detailed analysis on the CV results indicated that the capacitance of PCN-350 anode was mainly contributed to by a capacitive mechanism. The capacitive contribution increases from 68.3% to 82.7% when the scan rate rises from 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,c). It has been widely acknowledged that the capacitance-dominated Na-storage behavior is always favorable for the fast transfer of charge, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref42" aria-label="reference 1 and 42">(1,42)</a> which explains well the superior rate capability for the PCN-350 anode. Importantly, the distinctive slope-dominated Na storage far away from 0 V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) can effectively avoid the formation of Na dendrite on the surface of PCN-350 anode, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> ensuring a reliable and safe sodiation/desodiation process even at high current rates like 2C.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a–c) Quantitative analysis of capacitance-dominated Na storage for PCN-350: (a) CV curves at scan rates of 0.2–1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Contribution of capacitance process and diffusion process. (c) Contribution of capacitance process at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Electrochemical impedance spectra and the equivalent circuit for PCN-350 before and after cycling at 2 C. (e, f) SEM images of the PCN electrode over 6500 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The EIS of the PCN-350 anode before and after long-term cycling of 2800 and 4500 cycles were evaluated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d). A lower charge-transfer resistance for the cycled PCN-350 anode could be confirmed by the smaller radius of the semicircles in the high- to medium-frequency range. The decrease of the resistance was probably related to the electrochemical activation of the anode during the repeated charge/discharge. A detailed analyses on the Nyquist plots of the cycled PCN-350 anode with an equivalent circuit showed that there are two semicircular portions that appeared in the high- to middle-frequency ranges, corresponding to two charge-transfer resistances for two interfaces: the first one is for the electrolyte/SEI interface (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>), and the second one is for the SEI/electrode interface (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). We could observe that <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> raised from 105.4 Ω to 236.1 Ω, while <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> decreased from 463.8 Ω to 326.2 Ω when extending the cycling from 2800 to 4500 cycles. The sum values of the <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for the 2800 cycles PCN-350 and 4500 cycles are 569.2 Ω and 562.3 Ω, respectively, both of them being much lower than the resistances value (2688 Ω) of the original PCN-350 anode. Ex situ SEM evaluation on PCN-350 over 6500 cycles at 2C illustrated a well-preserved morphology of the anode film without serious cracks and obvious peeling of the active materials compared to the original anode film (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e,f, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), suggesting a good durability of the PCN-350 electrode for accommodating the volume deformation for outstanding stability.</div><div class="NLM_p">The extraordinary electrochemical performance of PCNs in half-cell NIBs inspired us to have a further evalution of its application potential in full-cell NIBs. The full cells were assembled by using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and PCN-350 sample as cathode and anode, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> showed the charge/discharge profile of the NVP cathode at 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> showed a typical charge/discharge curve of PCN-350//NVP configuration at 0.2C (60 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The reversible capacity for PCN-350 was 348 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The PCN-350//NVP full-cell battery delivered an initial open voltage around 3.2 V after charging and can simultaneously power up to 40 light-emitting diode (LED) bulbs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b,c). Equally importantly, the PCN-350//NVP full cell showed a fast and stable Na-storage capability.Typically, the reversible capacity is around 250 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at high current density of 2 C and keeps at 218 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles (0.07% capacity decay per cycle over 200 cycles at 2C) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d). The outstanding performance for the full cells suggested the promising potential PCN-350 anode for its future application.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/medium/am2c11132_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical characterization of the PCN-350 anode in NIBs full cell with commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as cathode. (a) Schematic illustration of the PCN-350//NVP NIBs full cell. (b) Open circuit voltage of PCN-NVP full cell after charging at 0.2 C. (c) 40 LED bulbs lit by the PCN-350//NVP NIBs full cell. (d) Cycling performance of the PCN-350//NVP NIBs full cell at 2 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/asset/images/large/am2c11132_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11132&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we identified a new and reliable moderate-pyrolysis strategy for building a high-performance polymer anode in NIBs. This moderate-pyrolysis strategy is simple, effective, and highly scalable but has been paid less attention to in the polymer electrode field. We demonstrated that such moderate pyrolysis could reduce the optical bandgap and optimize the microstructures of the PCN anode, thereby improving its electronic conductivity effectively and facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> transfer/storage significantly. We demonstrated that a suitable heating temperature (e.g., 350 °C) was extraordinarily vital for this moderate pyrolysis. The resultant PCN-350 anode was able to promise extraordinary electrochemical performance with a combination of high Na-storage capacity (351 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C), superior rate capability (95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20C), and ultrastable cyclability (&lt;0.0018% capacity decay per cycle over 6500 cycles at 2C). We also confirmed that the PCN-350 anode could promise high capacity and superb cycling stability in a full cell configuration with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as cathode. It is worth mentioning that only a reasonable fraction of conductive additive (10 wt %) is added for preparing the working electrodes of PCN, providing a highly potential for their future application. This finding opens a facile and effective avenue in building a robust polymer anode with enhanced electronic conductivity and desirable microstructure for high-capacity, fast, and stable sodium storage.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Preparation of PCN-X</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All chemicals and solvents for the material synthesis were purchased from J&amp;K and used without further purification. Hexaaminobenzene (HAB) trihydrochloride was synthesized according the reported literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 60 and 61">(60,61)</a> Pristine PCN was first synthesized via a simple polycondensation of hexaketocyclohexane (HKH) octahydrate and hexaaminobenzene (HAB) trihydrochloride. For a typical synthesis, HAB (1 mmol) and HKH (1 mmol) were charged in a two-necked round-bottom flask under argon atmosphere and placed in an ice bath. Deoxygenated NMP (20 mL) with a few drops of sulfuric acid was slowly added. The reaction flask was allowed to warm to room temperature for 2 h. The ice bath was replaced with an oil bath and heated to 180 °C for 12 h. Then, the flask was cooled to room temperature and water was added. The solid product that precipitated was collected by suction filtration using polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) (0.45 μm) membrane and thoroughly washed with water, methanol, and ether, respectively, and dried at 120 °C in vacuum for 24 h. Then PCN-Pristine was heated at different temperature (200 °C, 350 °C, 500 °C, and 700 °C) at heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in flowing Ar atmosphere for 2 h in a tube furnace. The obtained sample in the contribution named as PCN-X (X= pyrolysis temperature).</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Characterization Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology characterizations were measured by a transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEM2100F). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data were collected by a Thermo Scientific K-Alpha+. UV–vis absorption spectra were measured with a UV-2600 spectrophotometer (Shimadzu). The electronic conductivity was measured on the pressed pellet sample using four-contact probe method on a Keithley 2002 Multimeter (Keithley Instrument Inc.). A tape was horizontally laid on a polyimide (PI) film; then the powder sample was spread evenly over the tape and covered by another PI film. The PI films containing the sample were then compressed under a pressure of 10 MPa for about 10 s. A compacted sample film attached on the tape was obtained and cut to be cuboid film (5 mm × 10 mm), which was used for the electronic conductivity measurement. Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) patterns were obtained with an Ultima IV X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku) using Cu Ka irradiation (λ = 1.5406 Å). FTIR spectra were tested by Nicolet 6700. Raman spectra were recorded by HORIBA LabRAM HR Evolution (λ = 532 nm). TEM images were characterized by a JEOL-2100F microscope. SEM images were acquired on a FEI (Helios NanoLab G3 CX) microscope. TG-MS was performed on a STA449F3-QMS403C (Netzsch) under nitrogen atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">For electrochemical testing of PCN-350 Na half-cells, the electrochemical measurements were tested with CR2032 coin cells (half-cell) at room temperature. To prepare working electrodes, the active materials were mixed with Super-P and binder (CMC/SBR with mass ratio of 1:1) in a weight ratio of 80:10:10 to make a slurry. The slurry was then cast onto an Al foil and dried at 70 °C for 12 h in vacuum. The mass loading of the active material was about 0.6–1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. CR2032-type coil cells were assembled in a glovebox filled with argon gas (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O levels &lt; 0.1 ppm). Sodium metal (Sigma Ltd.) was used as counter electrode. A 1 M amount of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a 1:1 (volume ratio) mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) was used as electrolyte. Glass microfiber filter (Whatman) was used as separator. Charge/discharge tests were performed at different current densities on a LAND battery testing system (CT-3001A) at room temperature. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis were performed on an electrochemical workstation (Metrohm Autolab).</div><div class="NLM_p last">For lectrochemical testing of PCN-NVP full cells, the Na-ion full cells were constructed by using PCN as anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as cathode in a CR2032 coin-type cell; the full cells were charged and discharged in a voltage range of 0.5–3.5 V at room temperature. To prepare Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, the active materials were mixed with Super-P and binder (5% PVDF in NMP) in a weight ratio of 80:10:10 to make a slurry. The slurry was then cast onto an Al foil and dried at 110 °C for 12 h in vacuum. The weight ratio of the active materials of the anode and cathode was around 1:4, corresponding to an excessive capacity of ∼8.6% in the cathode side. Both the PCN-350 and the NVP cathodes were charged/discharged for two cycles before assembly of the PCN-NVP full cells. A 1 M amount of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a 1:1 (volume ratio) mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) was used as electrolyte. Whatman glass fiber was used as separator. Charge/discharge tests were performed at different current densities on the LAND battery testing system (CT-3001A) at room temperature.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c11132?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c11132</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">General procedures, XPS, SEM, EDS, TG-MS, and FT-IR of products, photograph of electrode, and summary of electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11132/suppl_file/am2c11132_si_001.pdf">am2c11132_si_001.pdf (2.79 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c11132" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                10.1126/science.aav5842</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D370%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D192-197%26journal%3DScience%26author%3DH.%2BJin%26author%3DS.%2BXin%26author%3DC.%2BChuang%26author%3DW.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BZhu%26author%3DH.%2BXie%26author%3DT.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BWan%26author%3DZ.%2BQi%26author%3DW.%2BYan%26author%3DY.-R.%2BLu%26author%3DT.-S.%2BChan%26author%3DX.%2BWu%26author%3DJ.%2BB.%2BGoodenough%26author%3DH.%2BJi%26author%3DX.%2BDuan%26title%3DBlack%2BPhosphorus%2BComposites%2Bwith%2BEngineered%2BInterfaces%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2BHigh-Capacity%2BLithium%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1126%252Fscience.aav5842&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c11132&amp;doiOfLink=10.1126%2Fscience.aav5842&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Black phosphorus composites with engineered interfaces for high-rate high-capacity lithium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Hongchang; Xin, Sen; Chuang, Chenghao; Li, Wangda; Wang, Haiyun; Zhu, Jian; Xie, Huanyu; Zhang, Taiming; Wan, Yangyang; Qi, Zhikai; Yan, Wensheng; Lu, Ying-Rui; Chan, Ting-Shan; Wu, Xiaojun; Goodenough, John B.; Ji, Hengxing; Duan, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">370</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6513</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">192-197</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1095-9203</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-rate lithium (Li) ion batteries that can be charged in minutes and store enough energy for a 350-mi driving range are highly desired for all-elec. vehicles.  A high charging rate usually leads to sacrifices in capacity and cycling stability.  The use is reported of black phosphorus (BP) as the active anode for high-rate, high-capacity Li storage.  The formation of covalent bonds with graphitic carbon restrains edge reconstruction in layered BP particles to ensure open edges for fast Li+ entry, the coating of the covalently bonded BP-graphite particles with electrolyte-swollen polyaniline yields a stable solid-electrolyte interphase and inhibits the continuous growth of poorly conducting Li fluorides and carbonates to ensure efficient Li+ transport.  The resultant composite anode demonstrates an excellent combination of capacity, rate, and cycling endurance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_iGAQqirQRbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhRFU6kzW9Irw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitVCrsbjP&amp;md5=cc3f720c4c8da9258eea0b038b7f916a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/s41563-020-0756-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Long, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miao, G.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moodera, J. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Extra Storage Capacity in Transition Metal Oxide Lithium-Ion Batteries Revealed by in Situ Magnetometry</span>. <i>Nat. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>20</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">76</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">83</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/s41563-020-0756-y</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D20%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D76-83%26journal%3DNat.%2BMater.%26author%3DQ.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BLi%26author%3DQ.%2BXia%26author%3DZ.%2BHu%26author%3DY.%2BZhu%26author%3DS.%2BYan%26author%3DC.%2BGe%26author%3DQ.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BShang%26author%3DS.%2BFan%26author%3DY.%2BLong%26author%3DL.%2BGu%26author%3DG.-X.%2BMiao%26author%3DG.%2BYu%26author%3DJ.%2BS.%2BMoodera%26title%3DExtra%2BStorage%2BCapacity%2Bin%2BTransition%2BMetal%2BOxide%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%2BRevealed%2Bby%2Bin%2BSitu%2BMagnetometry%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41563-020-0756-y&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c11132&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41563-020-0756-y&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extra storage capacity in transition metal oxide lithium-ion batteries revealed by in situ magnetometry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Qiang; Li, Hongsen; Xia, Qingtao; Hu, Zhengqiang; Zhu, Yue; Yan, Shishen; Ge, Chen; Zhang, Qinghua; Wang, Xiaoxiong; Shang, Xiantao; Fan, Shuting; Long, Yunze; Gu, Lin; Miao, Guo-Xing; Yu, Guihua; Moodera, Jagadeesh S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">76-83</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), many promising electrodes that are based on transition metal oxides exhibit anomalously high storage capacities beyond their theor. values.  Although this phenomenon has been widely reported, the underlying physicochem. mechanism in such materials remains elusive and is still a matter of debate.  In this work, we use in situ magnetometry to demonstrate the existence of strong surface capacitance on metal nanoparticles, and to show that a large no. of spin-polarized electrons can be stored in the already-reduced metallic nanoparticles (that are formed during discharge at low potentials in transition metal oxide LIBs), which is consistent with a space charge mechanism.  Through quantification of the surface capacitance by the variation in magnetism, we further show that this charge capacity of the surface is the dominant source of the extra capacity in the Fe3O4/Li model system, and that it also exists in CoO, NiO, FeF2 and Fe2N systems.  The space charge mechanism revealed by in situ magnetometry can therefore be generalized to a broad range of transition metal compds. for which a large electron d. of states is accessible, and provides pivotal guidance for creating advanced energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrX_3oIxgs2wrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lheGmsxv9ITaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhs1ehsrvM&amp;md5=6175baac970ba7492189f24907aa1051</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/s41893-020-00664-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yaghoobnejad

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Research Development on K-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hosaka, Tomooki; Kubota, Kei; Hameed, A. Shahul; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">120</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6358-6466</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Li-ion batteries (LIBs), commercialized in 1991, have the highest energy d. among practical secondary batteries and are widely utilized in electronics, elec. vehicles, and even stationary energy storage systems.  Along with the expansion of their demand and application, concern about the resources of Li and Co is growing.  Therefore, secondary batteries composed of earth-abundant elements are desired to complement LIBs.  In recent years, K-ion batteries (KIBs) have attracted significant attention as potential alternatives to LIBs.  Previous studies have developed pos. and neg. electrode materials for KIBs and demonstrated several unique advantages of KIBs over LIBs and Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  Thus, besides being free from any scarce/toxic elements, the low std. electrode potentials of K/K+ electrodes lead to high operation voltages competitive to those obsd. in LIBs.  Moreover, K+ ions exhibit faster ionic diffusion in electrolytes due to weaker interaction with solvents and anions than that of Li+ ions; this is essential to realize high-power KIBs.  This review comprehensively covers the studies on electrochem. materials for KIBs, including electrode and electrolyte materials and a discussion on recent achievements and remaining/emerging issues.  The review also includes insights into electrode reactions and solid-state ionics and nonaq. soln. chem. as well as perspectives on the research-based development of KIBs compared to those of LIBs and NIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoJrb33gTbyWrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lheGmsxv9ITaQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ copolymerizated gel polymer electrolyte with cross-linked network for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Niu, Yu-Bin; Yin, Ya-Xia; Wang, Wen-Peng; Wang, Peng-Fei; Ling, Wei; Xiao, Yao; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">CCS Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">589-597</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CCCHB2</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Chinese Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High thermal stability, nonflammability, and no liq. leakage are indispensable capabilities for electrolytes in sodium-ion batteries toward large-scale energy storage systems.  The use of solid-state or gel polymer electrolytes has proven to be one of the enabling tools to bring about these advancements; however, their application suffer from tedious synthesis procedure and/or lowionic transport to ensure a battery operation.  Herein, a novel gel polymer electrolyte with a cross-linked polyether network (GPE-CPN) was crafted through a self-catalyzed strategy, where in situ copolymn. of two monomers, 1,3-dioxolane and trimethylolpropane triglycidyl ether is realized successfully, with the use of sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF6) as an initiator, at room temp.  We demonstrate that the resultant GPE-CPN possesses a superior electrochem. stability window up to 4 V vs. Na+/Na, a considerable ionic cond., of 8.2 x 10-4S cm-1 at room temp., which is a capability good enough to suppress the growth of sodium dendrites and thus, stabilize the interface of electrolyte/sodium anode.  Considering the benefit from its facile fabrication and superior characteristics, the asgenerated GPE-CPN reveals a potential application for future rechargeable sodium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpC1ObdtRef27Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lheGmsxv9ITaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhslyls7bO&amp;md5=e117964a20bb8675c2dc9fd031cf418f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2022.140403"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meghnani, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, R. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Influence of Synthesis Route on the Structure and Electrochemical Performance of Biphasic (O′3/O3) NaNi<sub>0.815</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Al<sub>0.035</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>419</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">140403</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2022.140403</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D419%26publication_year%3D2022%26pages%3D140403%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DD.%2BMeghnani%26author%3DR.%2BK.%2BSingh%26title%3DInfluence%2Bof%2BSynthesis%2BRoute%2Bon%2Bthe%2BStructure%2Band%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BBiphasic%2B%2528O%25E2%2580%25B23%252FO3%2529%2BNaNi0.815Co0.15Al0.035O2%2Bcathode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2022.140403&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c11132&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2022.140403&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Influence of synthesis route on the structure and electrochemical performance of biphasic (O'3/O3) NaNi0.815Co0.15Al0.035O2 cathode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Meghnani, Dipika; Singh, Rajendra Kumar</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">419</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">140403</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have gained widespread attention for energy storage devices because of low cost and high abundance of sodium sources.  In this paper, Biphasic O'3/O3 layered NaNi0.815Co0.15Al0.035O2 cathode materials (Na-NCA) are synthesized by using three different chem. routes viz. copptn., hydrothermal and sol-gel methods.  X-ray diffraction pattern and Raman anal. show the clear evidence of layered structure of all three samples.  The kinetics of sodium ion [Na+] is deduced from cyclic voltammetry techniques and the values of Na+ ion diffusion coeff. is found to be 1.62 x 10-12, 1.11 x 10-13 and 1.71 x 10-12cm2s-1 for Na-NCA synthesized by copptn., sol-gel and hydrothermal methods, resp. implying the better kinetics of Na+- ions in hydrothermal prepd. Na-NCA cathode.  Furthermore, the Cyclic voltammetry and differential capacity vs. voltage (dQ/dV vs.V) studies reveal the good reversibility of phase transition in cathode prepd. using hydrothermal route.  Electrochem. testing indicates the better performance of hydrothermally prepd. Na-NCA in terms of discharge capacity, capacity retention and energy d.  This improved electrochem. performance is attributed to decease in solid electrolyte interface, reversible phase transition and high surface area as well as better diffusion coeff.  Addnl., evolution of phase after cycling process are also investigated and only single O'3 phase is obsd. for hydrothermal prepd.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXITTEG3ys2Q"> &gt;&gt; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, C.</span></span>;                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controlling the morphology, size and phase of Nb2O5 crystals for high electrochemical performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liao, Jiaqin; Tan, Rou; Kuang, Zhixiang; Cui, Chunyu; Wei, Zengxi; Deng, Xiaolan; Yan, Zhanheng; Feng, Yuezhan; Li, Fang; Wang, Caiyun; Ma, Jianmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chinese Chemical Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The morphol., size and phase of the material play a crucial role in its electrochem. performance.  Herein, the nano-sized niobium pentoxide (Nb2O5) with different morphologies and phase structures are synthesized through a very simple thermal treatment method, including the pseudohexagonal Nb2O5 nanosheets and pseudohexagonal Nb2O5 nanoparticles, orthorhombic Nb2O5 nanoparticles.  The synthesized pseudohexagonal Nb2O5 nanosheets and orthorhombic Nb2O5 nanoparticles exhibit better cycling and rate performance than the pseudohexagonal Nb2O5 nanoparticles due to the different morphologies and phase structures.  The T-Nb2O5-700 nanoparticles show the higher capacity (175 mAh/g) than that of TT-Nb2O5-500 nanosheets (127 mAh/g) and TT-Nb2O5-600 nanoparticles (39 mAh/g) at a c.d. of 50 mA/g and good rate performance with a capacity of 140 mAh/g at 1.0 A/g.  The excellent rate capability and cycling stability of orthorhombic T-Nb2O5 may be ascribed to the dominant contribution of pseudocapacitive effect.  This material has the great potential as a practical high-rate anode material for lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp9myqlLrSLSLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liXITTEG3ys2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXisVemsrbJ&amp;md5=7e29977a5f7a78efc0795d3bc9906429</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2020.147695"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mishra, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, S.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Surface modification of nano Na[Ni0.60Mn0.35Co0.05]O2 cathode material by dextran functionalized RGO via hydrothermal treatment for high performance sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mishra, Raghvendra; Singh, Shishir K.; Gupta, Himani; Srivastava, Nitin; Meghnani, Dipika; Tiwari, Rupesh K.; Patel, Anupam; Tiwari, Anurag; Tiwari, Vimal K.; Singh, Rajendra K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
